    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="fbeafcfe188341739f038586de71f656">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1685ee38-a1c9-4e81-9811-2fd159278cc7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d208959589" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hydrogen-bonded organic frameworks (HOFs) are considered as potential choice for future energy storage systems due to their adjustable chemistry, environment friendliness, and cost-effectiveness. In this study, structurally stabilized and aldehyde-tuned hydrogen-bonded organic frameworks (HOFs-8) are designed and prepared to contain arrayed electronegative sites for sodium-ion storage. Benefitting from the flexible hydrogen bond and unique structural symmetry, HOFs-8 can achieve efficient utilization of the active sites and fast transport of sodium ions and electrons. The HOFs-8 electrode exhibits an impressive lifespan of 5000 cycles at 3.66&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (20&nbsp;C). In situ Fourier Transform infrared spectroscopy (in situ FT-IR) and ex situ X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy (ex situ XPS) analyses are performed to illustrate the mechanism of sodium-ion storage involving aldehyde-tuned C═O. Additionally, flexible hydrogen bonds in HOFs materials with unique structural symmetries are investigated to elucidate the mechanism of hydrogen bonding for improving their electrochemical properties. Density functional theory (DFT) simulations verified that HOFs-8 has excellent&nbsp;Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, enabling it&nbsp;to demonstrate outstanding&nbsp;rate capability. This work offers insight into the design of new electrodes and improved HOFs, which are expected to have tremendous potential in energy storage systems.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314851-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314851-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Investigations into the fabrication of affordable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with extended lifespans and superior safety characteristics have become a prevalent topic among researchers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0001_R_d208959577e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nature has an abundance of sodium, with reserves being 420 times more abundant than lithium reserves.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0002_R_d208959577e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0003_R_d208959577e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Informat">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the development of SIBs can help alleviate resource shortages and ensure a stable supply of raw materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0004_R_d208959577e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0005_R_d208959577e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energ. Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SIBs, as well as being comparatively inexpensive, can reduce the theoretical cost by 30−40% compared to lithium batteries. Furthermore, they provide greater safety and faster charging performance and have a vast array of potential applications in energy storage, vehicles, and other markets.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0006_R_d208959577e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0007_R_d208959577e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Transition metal oxides, prussian blue compounds, and polyanionic compounds are currently the main electrode materials used in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0008_R_d208959577e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0011_R_d208959577e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Nano">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the use of these materials in SIB electrodes presents several challenges. Transition metal oxide electrodes usually exhibit poor cycle stability owing to lattice collapse. Prussian blue compounds suffer from severe capacity degradation, and polyanionic compounds show limited reversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0012_R_d208959577e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Science">12</a>-<a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0014_R_d208959577e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Carbon Neutralization">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the high cost and paucity of transition metal compounds have impeded the development of such materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0015_R_d208959577e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Lett.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, there is an immediate need to examine more promising materials (e.g., organic materials) to compensate for the inadequacy of the current materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0016_R_d208959577e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Mater. Today">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Organic materials have garnered increasing attention as alternative materials owing to their designable molecular structures, redox reactions with sodium ions, and substantial sodium storage capacities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0017_R_d208959577e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Small Methods">17</a>-<a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0019_R_d208959577e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Commun.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although small-molecule organics have considerable theoretical capacity, their randomly stacked structure in the collector limits sodium ion transport, and their high solubility in the electrolyte drastically restricts their use.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0020_R_d208959577e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ChemSusChem">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0021_R_d208959577e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Mater. Chem. A">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These solubility issues can be resolved through covalent bonding between organic monomers. The constructed conjugated polymers have low crystallinity, which makes it difficult for sodium storage functional groups to be exposed and the ions/electrons to be transferred correctly, thus constraining the desired electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0022_R_d208959577e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Asian. J.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0023_R_d208959577e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Power Sources">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast to covalent bonding, several organic monomers can self-assemble from weak hydrogen bonds, resulting in ordered porous structures, also referred to as hydrogen-bonded organic frameworks (HOFs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0024_R_d208959577e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0025_R_d208959577e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>HOFs are porous materials connected by hydrogen bonds and other interactions (e.g., π–π stacking and van der Waals forces) that have gained much attention since 1990.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0026_R_d208959577e645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">26</a>-<a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0028_R_d208959577e645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Commun.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to their extended π-conjugate structure, porosity, large specific surface area, abundance of active sites, etc., the usage of HOFs has been widespread in catalysts, gas storage, and sensors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0029_R_d208959577e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Cryst. Growth Des.">29</a>-<a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0032_R_d208959577e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These advantages suggest that HOFs can be used as electrode materials in SIBs. Unfortunately, there is not much knowledge about HOFs materials for energy storage. HOF relies mainly on weak hydrogen bonds, resulting in its structural instability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0033_R_d208959577e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Mater. Chem. A">33</a>-<a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0035_R_d208959577e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, when the metal ion is present in the hydrogen bond position during battery cycling, it counteracts the hydrogen bond acceptor, resulting in structural failure. Hence, it is currently difficult to use HOFs for energy storage. It is crucial to design highly stable HOFs that can withstand cycling and effectively transport ions and electrons. The incorporation of redox-active elements in HOFs is anticipated to generate fresh avenues of research in the realm of organic electrode materials. The strategic arrangement of redox active sites, coupled with hydrogen bonding and π–π interactions, results in robust HOF structures that exhibit remarkable chemical durability and effectively resist hydrolysis while cycling SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we designed and prepared a stabilized Hydrogen-bonded organic frameworks (HOFs-8)&nbsp;using the monomeric N1, N3, N5-tris(pyridine-4-yl)benzene-1,3,5-tricarboxamide (TPBTC) pyridine N-atoms as hydrogen-bonding acceptors and amide H-atoms as hydrogen-bonding donors. Each TPBTC molecule was connected to three other TPBTC molecules through three sets of highly symmetrical hydrogen bonds, then forming a super-molecular layer porous structure. The unbonded C═O is a useful storage functional group. For comparison, we also developed hydrogen-bonded organic aromatic frameworks (HOAFs) with C═O functional groups. The HOFs-8 electrode displays an initial reversible capacity of 172.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an elevated coulombic efficiency of 96.6%. The HOFs-8 electrodes demonstrated exceptional high-rate cycling stability, preserving their reversible capacities of 102.4 and 70.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 5000 cycles at current densities of 10&nbsp;C (1.83&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and 20&nbsp;C (3.66&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), respectively. The outstanding electrochemical performance of HOFs-8 electrodes was attributed to their high sodium ion diffusion rate. Furthermore, the elastic hydrogen bonds incorporated into the HOFs-8 structure inhibited the dissolution of organic molecules, enhanced the structural integrity, and diminished volume growth, thereby guaranteeing long-term stability. Theoretical calculations indicate that Na<sup>+</sup> has rapid reaction kinetics in HOFs-8. In addition, the sodium storage functional group of HOFs-8 was identified as C═O through in situ Fourier Transform infrared spectroscopy (in situ FT-IR), ex situ X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy (ex situ XPS). This study aimed to demonstrate the design of high-performance green organic electrodes for rechargeable batteries to inform the further development of HOF materials for energy storage applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314851-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314851-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314851-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314851-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials Preparation and Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The synthesis of HOFs-8 is depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0001">1a</a>. At 0&nbsp;°C, the N1, N3, N5-tris(pyridine-4-yl) benzene-1,3,5-tri carboxamide (TPBTC) molecule was synthesized by combining acetyl chloride (1,3,5-benzotricarbonyl chloride) and 4-aminopyridine organic amine monomer, using tetrahydrofuran (THF) as the reaction solvent. Triethylamine was employed as an acid-binding agent to ensure that the reaction proceeded properly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0036_R_d208959577e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, the organic small molecule TPBTC, fabricated using a combination of trichloromethane and methanol, self-assembled to form a hydrogen-bonded system HOFs-8. Each TPBTC molecule was connected to three other TPBTC molecules through three sets of highly symmetrical hydrogen bonds (N─H─N, 2.166&nbsp;Å), which produced a 2D supermolecular layer along the ac plane. Then, the layers were arranged via π–π interaction in the c-axis direction to construct a 3D micropore supermolecular framework (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0001">1c</a>). The resulting product was a yellow-brown powder (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). A graphical representation of the procedure for synthesizing the hydrogen-bonded organic aromatic frameworks (HOAFs)&nbsp;is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0001">1b</a>. By using NaH as a non-nucleophilic base and THF as the reaction solvent, benzene-1,3,5-triyltris((9H-carbazol-9-yl)methanone (PhTCz) was synthesized from 1,3,5-benzotricarbonyl chloride and carbazole monomers at 0&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0037_R_d208959577e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After, HOAFs with porous structures were produced via incubation in a mixture of dichloromethane and ethyl acetate at ambient temperature. These HOAFs were joined by intra-layer π–π stacking and interlayer hydrogen bonds (C═O─H, see also Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0001">1d</a>). The π–π interaction forces and hydrogen bonding (eg., N─H─N and C═O─H) in the HOFs structure contributed to its structural stability. Furthermore, the porous structure of HOFs-8 and HOAFs, enabled electrolyte and ionic transport, and the C═O active functional groups present in the HOFs structure can be utilized as active sites for sodium-ion storage, making HOFs a viable electrode material for SIBs.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314851-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bcdd886a-54b6-4604-b4b2-ab46a4112b61/adfm202314851-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bcdd886a-54b6-4604-b4b2-ab46a4112b61/adfm202314851-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/777dfc4c-d9e3-4d74-bdf7-98233ed74d84/adfm202314851-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bcdd886a-54b6-4604-b4b2-ab46a4112b61/adfm202314851-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314851-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314851" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram illustration of the formation procedure. a) HOFs-8; b) HOAFs; c) 3D supermolecular microporous structure of HOFs-8 by Hydrogen bonding interactions based on TPBTC molecules; d) Molecular stacking structure of HOAFs by hydrogen-bonded aromatic based on PhTCz molecules.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) spectra of HOFs-8 exhibited distinct diffraction peaks at 7.1°, 8.4°, 11.1°, and 13.2° (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0002">2a</a>). The characteristic peaks of the simulated XRD pattern of HOFs-8 based on the molecular structure were consistent, confirming the successful preparation of HOFs-8. In the FT-IR spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0002">2b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), the peaks at 1330 and 1685&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to the vibrations of C─N and C═O in HOFs-8, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0038_R_d208959577e754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano">38</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0039_R_d208959577e754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison to TPBTC, the peak at 2938&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> was absent in HOFs-8, indicating that HOFs-8 created an ordered structure and the N─H─O hydrogen bond was eliminated. Notably, a stretching vibration was observed at 3070 and 3240&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> for the N─H─N bond, showing the presence of hydrogen bonds in HOFs-8.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0040_R_d208959577e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The HOAF illustrated peaks at 1608, 1451, and 1330&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, indicating C═O, C═C, and C─N, respectively. Thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA) indicated that HOFs-8 and HOAF have remarkable thermal stability, and their decomposition temperatures reached 305 and 380&nbsp;°C, respectively. The loss weight drops slightly from room temperature as the solvent is removed from the framework (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0002">2c</a>). Additionally, the XRD spectra of HOF-8 heated to 260&nbsp;°C did not show any significant change (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The exceptional thermal resistance of HOFs-8 can be attributed to its supramolecular framework, which consists of three sets of symmetrical hydrogen bonds. Besides, the HOFs-8 exhibits superior chemical stability in the electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). And the major functional groups remained unchanged after electrolyte immersion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, HOFs-8 and HOAFs showed type-I adsorption/desorption isotherm curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0002">2d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). A sharp nitrogen uptake was observed when P/P<sub>0</sub> was less than 0.05, indicating the existence of micropores (as displayed in the included image, the primary pore sizes of HOFs-8 and HOAFs are concentrated at 0.7 and 1.2&nbsp;nm).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0041_R_d208959577e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The HOFs-8 exhibited a large pore volume of 0.749&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. According to the Brunauer-Emmett-Teller method, the surface area of HOFs-8 and HOAFs were estimated to be 938 and 362&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of HOFs-8 revealed a layered structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0002">2f,g</a>). In contrast, ball-milled HOFs-8 exhibited an ultrathin, virtually transparent laminar structure with a considerable reduction in size and thinness. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0002">2h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Atomic force microscopy (AFM) characterization indicated that the ball-milled HOFs-8 were extremely thin, between 2 and 5&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0002">2e</a>). The stratified structure expedites the movement of electrons and ions, leading to enhanced reaction kinetics of the electrode and resulting in improved electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0042_R_d208959577e819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> HOAFs exhibited a long-form blocky morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). And the lattice spacing of HOFs-8 is displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0002">2i</a>, where the accurate interplanar distance can be calculated to be 1.05&nbsp;nm. Furthermore, through HAADF-TEM imaging, it is evident that C, N, and O elements are evenly distributed within the HOFs-8 (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0002">2j–m</a>).</p>

               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314851-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e6f62067-349a-42de-81d2-be7841aa22be/adfm202314851-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e6f62067-349a-42de-81d2-be7841aa22be/adfm202314851-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3e8e4d60-e62b-4528-ae30-4eeb95f4e0f8/adfm202314851-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e6f62067-349a-42de-81d2-be7841aa22be/adfm202314851-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314851-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314851" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of HOFs-8 (insert image: molecular structure of HOFs-8); b) FT-IR spectrum of HOFs-8 and HOAFs in comparison with TPBTC and PhTCz molecules; c) TG analysis of HOFs-8 and HOAFs; d) Nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherm of HOFs-8 and HOAFs; e) The AFM image of the HOFs-8 and the corresponding height profile; f) SEM image of HOFs-8; g) TEM image of HOFs-8; h) TEM images of HOFs-8 after ball-milled; i) The lattice spacing of HOFs-8; j) HAADF-TEM of HOFs-8; The corresponding TEM element mapping images of HOFs-8 k) C, l) N, m) O.</div>

               </figure>
            </section>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314851-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314851-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The performances of HOFs-8, HOAFs, TPBTC, and PhTCz as working electrodes for SIBs were assessed. Applying the formula <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/422ee586-42e9-4a23-b342-2837a88f6375/adfm202314851-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314851:adfm202314851-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202314851-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>Q</mi>
                        <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        <mo>=</mo>
                        <mfrac>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>n</mi>
                              <mi>F</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <mrow>
                              <mn>3.6</mn>
                              <mi>M</mi>
                           </mrow>
                        </mfrac>
                        <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$Q\ = \frac{{nF}}{{3.6M}}\ $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0043_R_d208959577e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Informat">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the theoretical capacity of HOFs-8 and HOAFs was calculated as 183.7 and 122.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0003">3a</a> illustrates the cycling performance of samples at 55&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (0.3&nbsp;C). The initial reversible capacity of the HOFs-8 electrodes (167.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) exceeded that of the pristine TPBTC (28.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and the coulombic efficiency (CE) was also higher (96.6%). Moreover, the HOFs-8 electrode exhibited outstanding electrochemical and structural constancy with a considerable discharge capacity of 150.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> maintained after 200 cycles. The superior electrochemical performance of the HOF-8 electrode was mainly attributed to its lamellar porous channel structure, which facilitated electrolyte infiltration and electron and ion transport. Concurrently, ball-milled HOFs-8 offers adequate exposure of the active sites for sodium ions storage, increasing the reaction kinetics, and consequently, the electrochemical performance is improved.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0044_R_d208959577e904" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Membr. Sci.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the elastic hydrogen-bonded structure in HOFs-8 successfully restricted the structural deterioration during cycling, which preserved HOFs morphology, thereby improving the lasting cycling performance of the HOFs-8 electrodes. The SEM and TEM images in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information) show that the HOFs-8 maintained its lamellar architecture within 200 cycles. The HOAF electrode showed a noteworthy initial discharge capacity of 120.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. However, this capacity rapidly diminished in subsequent cycles because of the binding of sodium ions to the hydrogen bond acceptor (C═O) in the HOAFs structure, which led to irreversible destruction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves indicate that the HOFs-8 electrode stabilized rapidly after its initial discharge (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0003">3b</a>). Furthermore, it displayed a gradually sloping curve throughout the sodiation and desodiation cycles with an average potential of ≈2.2&nbsp;V, corresponding to the C═O functional group for Na<sup>+</sup> storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0045_R_d208959577e923" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Rapid Commun.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Rate capability is an essential index of electrochemical performance. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0003">3c</a> illustrates the rate capabilities of HOFs-8. Despite rapid changes in current density, the HOFs-8 electrode maintained the most reliable cycling performance and highest capacity at each current rate. At current densities of 0.3, 0.6, 1, 3, 5, and 10&nbsp;C, the average capacity of the HOFs-8 electrode was 158.8, 148.8, 140.6, 132.5, 122.3, and 107.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The capacity of HOFs-8 was measured to be 154.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after being restored to 0.3&nbsp;C, which attested to the simplicity of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. In particular, the voltage plateau of the HOFs-8 electrodes at various current densities remained approximately the same, which suggests that the material has a fast redox capability when Na<sup>+</sup> are inserted/extracted quickly and maintain their structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0003">3d</a>). In comparison, the HOAFs electrode demonstrates poor rate capacities with low capacities and coulombic efficiency under the same test conditions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0003">3e</a>). Surprisingly, the HOFs-8 electrode presented an extraordinary high-rate cycling stability, maintaining its discharge capacity of 102.4 and 70.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 5000 cycles at 10 and 20&nbsp;C current densities, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0003">3f</a>). The voltage plateaus presented by GCD curves remain basically the same in 5000 cycles, which indicates that the HOFs-8 electrodes have a stable charging and discharging performance at a large rate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the redox behavior of HOFs-8 was then evaluated through cyclic voltammetry (CV). Its CV curves demonstrated two distinct redox peaks (2.48 and 2.09&nbsp;V), which was in agreement with the plateau displayed by the charge/discharge curve (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0003">3g</a>). Remarkably, the CV curves overlapped from the second cycle onward, suggesting a stable redox process for the HOFs-8 electrode. The redox behaviors of HOAFs were also tested through CV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0003">3h</a>). In addition, the HOFs-8 electrode shows superior electrochemical performance compared to most other organic electrode materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0003">3i</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314851-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/be6f84cf-0a74-4d5c-952a-0c0254e64bb0/adfm202314851-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/be6f84cf-0a74-4d5c-952a-0c0254e64bb0/adfm202314851-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bf0e5eb6-9b09-4a0c-8571-ca00dcd68825/adfm202314851-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/be6f84cf-0a74-4d5c-952a-0c0254e64bb0/adfm202314851-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314851-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314851" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performances of HOFs-8, HOAFs, TPBTC, and PhTCz at 0.3 C; b) Discharge/Charge curves of HOFs-8 electrode at 0.3&nbsp;C; c) Rate capabilities of HOFs-8 and HOAFs at different current rates from 0.3 to 10&nbsp;C; d) Discharge/Charge curves of d) HOFs-8 electrode, e) HOAFs electrode at various current densities of 0.3–10&nbsp;C; f) Cycling stability of HOFs-8 electrode at a large current of 10 and 20&nbsp;C; C–V curves of g) HOFs-8 electrode, h) HOAFs electrode at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> between 1.2 and 3.2&nbsp;V; i) Overview of key properties of HOFs-8 and other organic electrode materials.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In-situ FT-IR was employed to study the sodium storage mechanism in HOFs-8 (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0004">4a,b</a>). An empty backdrop was established as the initial situation, which was utilized to display the functional groups changes in vibrational intensity during the cycling process. The descending or ascending slope of the FT-IR peak intensity of the HOFs-8 electrode implies the disappearance of the original functional groups or the emergence of new functional groups, respectively. During the discharge process, the C═O functional groups at 1685&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> show a decrease in peak intensities, while the C─O peaks located at 1260&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> become more apparent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0046_R_d208959577e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem. Eng. J.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These findings suggest that carbonyl groups interact with sodium ions during discharge. The carbonyl groups experience a slow but steady return to their peaks during the charging cycle, suggesting a reversible storage system for sodium ions in carbonyl groups. Besides, the characteristic peak approximately at 1550&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is derived from the C─N stretching vibration. Moreover, the N─H group peak (3250–3050&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) underwent a periodic fluctuation in peak intensity throughout discharge/charge processes, indicating that the N─H─N hydrogen bonds in the HOFs structure are able to adapt flexibly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0047_R_d208959577e1012" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of elastic hydrogen bonds in HOFs-8 structures helps to decrease volume expansion during cycling, ultimately leading to improved stability of the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0048_R_d208959577e1018" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314851-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/20d34e12-a431-48e5-8ff5-b622e115d6c0/adfm202314851-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/20d34e12-a431-48e5-8ff5-b622e115d6c0/adfm202314851-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6face57a-f98c-49a3-8d2d-11594a040d76/adfm202314851-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/20d34e12-a431-48e5-8ff5-b622e115d6c0/adfm202314851-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314851-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314851" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Diagrammatic sketch of the In situ FT-IR device; b) In situ FT-IR spectra of HOFs-8 during the first fully discharged (sodiation) process to 1.2&nbsp;V and the first fully charged (desodiation) process to 3.2&nbsp;V; Ex situ XPS results of the c) N 1s, d) C 1s, and e) O 1s spectrum during the first fully discharged process to 1.2&nbsp;V and the first fully charged process to 3.2&nbsp;V; f) Schematic illustration of the sodiation/desodiation process of the HOFs-8 electrode; g) XRD spectra of the pristine HOFs-8, HOFs-8 initially discharged to 1.2&nbsp;V and subsequently charged to 3.2&nbsp;V; h–j) HRTEM images of the HOFs-8 at different voltages (during the second fully discharged process to 1.2&nbsp;V and subsequently fully charged process to 3.2&nbsp;V).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>An ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (ex situ XPS) test was employed to uncover the sodium ion storage mechanism of HOFs-8 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0004">4c–e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The N 1s spectra demonstrate the C─N and C═N bonds appearing at 399.2 and 298.5&nbsp;eV,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0049_R_d208959577e1050" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which remain consistent even during the discharge/charge cycle, suggesting that neither of these groups is involved in sodium storage. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0004">4d</a> shows the pristine HOFs-8 has five partial C 1s peaks at 288.9, 286.1, 285.3, 284.4, and 283.4&nbsp;eV, which is related to the C═O, C─N, C─C, C═C, and C─N bonds respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0050_R_d208959577e1059" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Small">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon discharging to 1.2&nbsp;V, the ratio of peak area for C─O and C═O showed a substantial increase. A gradual increase in the C═O/C─O peak area ratio as charging progressed (from 1.2&nbsp;V to 2&nbsp;V to 3.2&nbsp;V) confirmed the involvement of the C═O group in sodium storage. The XPS results of O 1s further validated the aforementioned results, the peaks centered around 534.8, 533.1, and 531.4&nbsp;eV were identified as C═O, C─N, and C═N bonds, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0004">4e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0051_R_d208959577e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Sci.">51</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0052_R_d208959577e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When pristine HOFs-8 was discharged to 1.2&nbsp;V, a substantial increase was recorded in the peak area ratio of C─O/C═O, demonstrating that the C═O bond was destroyed during the mediation process and the C─O─Na bond was generated. During charging (from 1.2&nbsp;V to 2&nbsp;V to 3.2&nbsp;V), the C═O/C─O peak area ratio increased, demonstrating the restoration of the C═O functional group. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0004">4f</a> illustrates the sodiation/desodiation process of HOFs-8, which shows the three highly symmetrical C═O functional groups in each organic monomer that builds the HOFs-8 molecular structure that reversibly reacts with sodium ions. XRD was also used to examine the molecular structure of HOFs-8, and the sodium salt was generated when HOFs-8 was discharged from the starting point to 1.2&nbsp;V. Subsequently, the distinctive peaks of HOFs-8 were regained after being recharged to 3.2&nbsp;V, demonstrating that HOFs-8 could reversibly accommodate sodium ions and its structure remained (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0004">4g</a>). The increased intensity of the 27<sup>o</sup> peak of the fully charged HOFs-8 may be due to the electrolyte entering the pores of HOFs-8 during the cycling process. This suggests that the HOFs-8 possesses exceptional electrolyte wettability, greatly enhancing its electrochemical performance. The ex situ SEM reveals that the HOFs-8 electrode undergoes a reversible interaction with sodium cations in the electrolyte during cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The HRTEM images of different discharge states indicate that a small amount of Na<sup>+</sup> is inserted in the HOFs-8 layer (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0004">4h–j</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0053_R_d208959577e1090" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Carbon Energy">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The remarkable cycling and rate capabilities of HOFs-8 merit our attention, and the sodium kinetics should be analyzed to determine the cause. First, the diffusion properties of Na<sup>+</sup> in the samples were probed using the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0005">5a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0054_R_d208959577e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Electrochim. Acta">54</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0055_R_d208959577e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J Electrochem. Soc.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The D<i><sub>Na+</sub></i> of the HOFs-8 electrode (1.1&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup> to 9.9&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−13</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>) is the largest of all samples, with a diffusion coefficient that exceeds that of its monomer sample (TPBTC) by about three orders of magnitude. This confirms that the uniform lamellar structure of the HOFs-8 sample is highly beneficial for sodium ion and electron transport and yields optimal electrochemical performance. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was conducted to validate internal electrochemical resistance and sodium ion diffusion coefficients.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0056_R_d208959577e1127" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Nyquist plots of the samples taken before cycling in the open circuit voltage state are illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). In the higher and middle-frequency areas, each graph consists of a semi-circle, whereas in the lower-frequency region, an inclined line is drawn. HOFs-8 exhibits the lowest charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>), demonstrating improved electronic conductivity and reduced inner electrochemical resistance. Additionally, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients were calculated by the Warburg regional equation: <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b44dc3c6-37bb-40bd-9d14-a62555a2945d/adfm202314851-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314851:adfm202314851-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202314851-math-0002.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>D</mi>

                              <mi>N</mi>

                                 <mi>a</mi>

                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                        </msub>

                           </mrow>

                           </mrow>

                        <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{N{a}^ + } = \frac{{{R}^2{T}^2}}{{2{A}^2{n}^4{F}^4{C}^2\sigma _{{\omega}}^2}}\ $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0057_R_d208959577e1181" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> σ<sub>ω</sub> can be attained from the slope of the fitting line of the EIS data at low frequencies: <i>Z</i>′ = <i>R<sub>c</sub></i>  + <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> + σ<sub>ω</sub> · ω<sup>−0.5</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0058_R_d208959577e1203" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Compos. Part B-Eng.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the HOFs-8 electrodes were characterized by greater Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients and more rapid dynamic characteristics than the other electrodes. This can probably explain why HOFs-8 had a remarkable rate capability and the highest reversible capacity.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314851-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0eee322f-7007-4cb9-8d43-a767fe3c85b1/adfm202314851-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0eee322f-7007-4cb9-8d43-a767fe3c85b1/adfm202314851-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b09476d3-586d-4809-b7ee-b32d5df3a660/adfm202314851-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0eee322f-7007-4cb9-8d43-a767fe3c85b1/adfm202314851-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314851-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314851" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) GITT curves and calculated sodium ion diffusion coefficients of HOFs-8 electrode; b) C–V curves at different scan rates ranging from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> of HOFs-8; c) Capacitive contribution at a scan rate of 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>; d) Contribution ratio of capacitive and diffusion controlled behaviors at various scan rates for HOFs-8 electrode; e) The molecular electrostatic potential of HOFs-8; f) The molecular electrostatic potential of HOFs-8 combined with three Na<sup>+</sup>; Calculated LUMO value g) and HOMO value h) of HOFs-8 based on the DFT calculation; i) Adsorption sites of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in HOFs-8; j) Na-O distance in HOFs-8; Simulated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion within HOFs-8, k) Schematic showing the P1 diffusion path, l) Schematic showing the P2 diffusion path; m) Corresponding activation energy profile.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>A representation of the CV of the HOFs-8 electrode is given in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0005">5b</a>, where it has been studied at different scan rates (0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>). Regardless of the scan rate, the shape of the curve remains constant. The mechanism of sodium storage can be deduced by analyzing the correlation between the measured current (<i>i</i>) and the scan rate (<i>v</i>), as expressed in the equations: <i>i</i>  =  <i>a</i> · <i>v<sup>b</sup></i>, log (<i>i</i>) =  <i>b</i> · log(<i>v</i>) + log(<i>a</i>), where <i>v</i> denotes the scan rate, <i>i</i> stands for the current, and both a and <i>b</i> (range from 0.5 to 1) are constants.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0059_R_d208959577e1279" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv. Sci.">59</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0060_R_d208959577e1279" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - ACS Nano">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The battery's behavior during discharging/charging process, can be determined by matching the scan rate to the peak current response. If the value of b is in the range of 0.5–1, the electrode material exhibits battery properties and pseudocapacitance properties. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15d</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrates the correlation between log <i>i</i> and log <i>v</i>. The <i>b</i> value range was calculated to be 0.53 and 0.58 for oxidization and reduction, implying both conversion reaction and capacitive behavior during cycling, and the total storage capacity is dominated by the diffusion-controlled faradaic process. The capacitive capacity was calculated further using the following equation: <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f0face92-7cc1-46df-b504-cd8bd4203cd0/adfm202314851-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314851:adfm202314851-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202314851-math-0003.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>i</mi>
                        <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        <mrow>
                           <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                           <mi>V</mi>
                           <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
                        </mrow>
                        <mo>=</mo>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>k</mi>
                           <mn>1</mn>
                        </msub>
                        <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        <mi>v</mi>
                        <mo>+</mo>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>k</mi>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                        </msub>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                           <mfrac>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </mfrac>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$i\ ( V ) = {k}_1\ v + {k}_2{v}^{\frac{1}{2}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>, where <i>i</i>(<i>V</i>) represents the measured current at a fixed potential (<i>v</i>), <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are adjustable constant parameters, and <i>v</i> denotes the scan rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0061_R_d208959577e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Chem. Eng. J.">61</a>, <a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0062_R_d208959577e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Nat. Commun.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> An increase in the scan rate leads to a higher capacitive contribution to HOFs-8, which signifies the existence of the surface absorption mechanism and active spots of the HOFs-8. Upon increasing the <i>v</i> value to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, the capacitive capacity contribution reached approximately 74.1% for the HOFs-8 electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0005">5c,d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). These factors enabled HOFs-8 to exhibit rapid charge transfer, high capacity, and good cyclability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0063_R_d208959577e1355" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Carbon Neutralization">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The possible redox-active sites were identified through simulation of the molecular electrostatic potential (MESP). The negative region, indicated by the blue color, is primarily located at the edges of the C═O bonds in the HOFs-8 sample, which suggests the C═O group is more reactive toward the positive Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0005">5j</a>). Moreover, the HOFs-8 units maintain its structural stability when accepting three sodium atoms. The MESP image showed that the HOFs-8 unit was more electronegative in its central benzene ring after binding three Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0005">5k</a>). This interaction reduces the diffusion pathway of ions and enhances the efficiency of redox reactions. Later, the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into various active sites of HOFs-8 units was simulated to obtain optimized geometries. The adsorption energy is calculated to be −1.814&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0005">5e</a>) with the Na-O distance of 1.513, 1.672, and 1.825&nbsp;Å (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0005">5f</a>). The HOFs-8 has a strong adsorption for Na<sup>+</sup>, enabling effective adsorption and storage. Additionally, the computed HOMO/LUMO orbital energies of HOFs-8 were determined to be −0.24472/−0.06782&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0005">5l,m</a>). The low HOMO and LUMO values promoted rapid ion/electron transfer, leading to improved conductivity and electrochemical properties of HOF materials in SIBs. Furthermore, the extent of Na<sup>+</sup> movement in the electrode material has a considerable influence on the rate performance of the battery. The movement of Na<sup>+</sup> in HOFs-8 was determined using theoretical calculations. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0005">5g,h</a>, two representative paths (P1 and P2) are demonstrated respectively. According to the calculated results, the diffusion barriers for P1 and P2 are 0.27 and 0.40&nbsp;eV, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314851-fig-0005">5i</a>). Compared with the diffusion rates of Na<sup>+</sup> in other organic or inorganic electrode materials, these two values are substantially lower.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314851-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314851-bib-0064_R_d208959577e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Small">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> HOF-8's low diffusion barrier and low diffusion resistance enable it to show exceptional rate capability and impressive stability during long-term high-rate cycling.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314851-sec-0050">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314851-sec-0050-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, chemically stable and high-performance hydrogen-bonded organic frameworks were designed to promote the application of organic electrodes in sodium-ion storage, and the storage mechanisms of HOFs were investigated. The HOFs-8 electrode exhibited a high reversible capacity of 172.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 55&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with an impressive capacity retention of 88.5% after 200 cycles. In particular, it enables an exceptional cycle life of 5000 cycles at 3.66&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is superior to most previously reported organic electrode materials. Theoretical simulations indicated that the HOFs-8&nbsp;has excellent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics within the organic frameworks, as verified by the HOMO-LUMO level. Moreover, in situ FT-IR and ex situ XPS were conducted to demonstrate the sodium storage mechanism of the HOFs-8 electrodes. The flexible hydrogen bonds and unique structural symmetry of these frameworks provide new insights into electrode design and extend the application of organic electrodes for alkali metal-ion batteries in the next generation large-scale energy storage systems.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
